                         <div id="articleHeader">
    <div id="main-article-info">
        <h1>Your brain may control your computer, but who's controlling your brain?</h1>
        <p id="stand-first" class="stand-first-alone">You wouldn't want a computer to unquestioningly act on what's inside your head</p>
    </div>
</div>

                         <div id="content"><ul class="article-attributes"><li> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/profile/charliebrooker"> <img class="contributor-pic-small" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/contributor/2007/09/28/charlie_brooker_140x140.jpg" width="60" height="60" alt="Charlie Brooker" title="Contributor picture" /> </a> </li> <li id="contrib-shift"><ul> <li class="byline"> <a class='contributor' href='http://www.guardian.co.uk/profile/charliebrooker'> Charlie Brooker</a> </li> <li class="publication"> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/theguardian" >The Guardian</a>, <time datetime="2011-04-11" >Monday 11 April 2011</time> </li> </ul></li> </ul> 
<div id="article-wrapper" data-global-auto-refresh-switch="on">
<div id="main-content-picture"><img src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/About/General/2011/4/8/1302285162577/Wired-to-computers-001.jpg" alt="Wired to computers" width="210" height="330" /> </a> <span class="caption" style="width: 210px;"> Resist the mind probe. Thicken your skull. Staple a doormat around it if necessary. Photograph: Getty Images/Science Photo Library </span> </span> <p>Ever since about 1998, when humankind began fast- forwarding through the gradually-unfolding history of progress, like someone impatiently zipping through a YouTube clip in search of the best bits, we've grown accustomed to machines veering from essential to obsolete in the blink of a trimester. VHS, the Walkman, fax machines, CD-Roms, pagers, dial-up modems . . . all consigned to the same wing of the museum housing the mangle and the horse-drawn plough.</p><p>The junk mountain grows by the day. If your home is anything like mine, it contains several rarely explored crannies stashed full of archaic chargers, defunct cables, and freshly antiquated gizmos whose sole useful function in 2011 is to make 2005 feel like 1926, simply by looking big and dull and impossibly lumpen. Everyone's opened a drawer and been startled by the unexpected discovery of an old mobile phone that now resembles an outsized pantomime prop. To think you used to be impressed by this clunky breezeblock. You were like a caveman gawping at a yo-yo.</p><p>Now it's almost time to hurl another outmoded device down the historical garbage chute: your body. Last week, researchers at <a href="http://news.wustl.edu/news/Pages/21876.aspx" title="Washington University ">Washington University </a>unveiled a new mind-control computer system. Traditional mind-control systems – and the fact that any mind-control system can be referred to as "traditional" shows you how nuts-deep into the future we already are – require the user to don an EEG skullcap before thinking very hard about specific actions. The resultant brainwaves are then crudely interpreted and the device reacts accordingly. But practical use is severely restricted thanks to the human skull, which muffles some signals and amplifies others. It's like trying to work out what your neighbours are up to by pressing your ear against the wall: fun, but often wildly misleading.</p><p>Which is where <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrocorticography" title="electrocorticography">electrocorticography</a> comes in. Electrocorticography basically means "sticking sensors directly on to the surface of the brain". Once you've done that, you get a far more reliable signal. Already they've had volunteers controlling an onscreen cursor by imagining different vowel sounds. As soon as they refine it further, giving the user the ability to steer the pointer around and click on things, the days of mass-market Wi-Fi mind-controlled iPads will be upon us before you can smother your kids in their sleep to protect them from precisely such a future.</p><p>But is this really so sinister? All computers are mind-controlled already. My hand may steer the mouse and my fingers may punch the keys, but none of this takes place without my mental say-so. My brain runs things round here. Surely all a mind-controlled interface does is cut out the corporeal middleman, leaving your fingers free to do something more useful, such as plugging your ears so you can't hear the horrified screams spontaneously exploding from your facehole? What's the problem?</p><p>The problem is that the body is the final, crucial buffer between the skittish human mind and the slavish machine servant. Think of how many furious email responses you've composed in haste, only to halt and reflect at the final moment as your finger hovers over the "send" button. The simple fact that a small physical action is required to actually deliver the damn thing is often enough to give pause for thought.</p><p>When mind-controlled computers become a commonplace reality, you'll have typed and sent that message in the time it takes to stub a toe; as quick as pulling a facial expression, but more detailed, and full of swearwords.</p><p>And while your brain might be great at controlling machines, how great are you at controlling your brain? What if, 10 years in the future, you're watching a cartoon on your futuristic 3D computer television, and the cartoon's got a rabbit in it, and the rabbit's slightly coquettish and flirty, and the knowing way it flicks that cotton tail as it hops makes you think about sex momentarily, and before you know it, your brain's retrieved some disgraceful bestial rabbit porn from the very worst corners of the Ultranet, and is relaying it on the display in lurid 96-inch holographic guttervision just as your wife and kid come back from the shops? And then, drunk on self-destructive power, your computer-mind takes a four-second video-snapshot of your own child's horrified gasping face and mischievously scribbles a load of penises and swastikas all over it, and uploads this vandalised looping portrait to your 3D holographic Facebook page accompanied by a headline screaming WITNESS MY NADIR – JUDGE ME! JUDGE ME! JUDGE ME ONE AND ALL!</p><p>Let's face it, if you're honest, there's a whole world of shit routinely fizzing and popping around in your head that you wouldn't want a computer to unquestioningly act on. Remember: when they triumphantly unveil an iPhone that lets you dial your sweetheart simply by thinking about their face, don't be fooled into thinking it's wonderful. It's a slippery slope. Resist the mind probe. Thicken your skull. Staple a doormat around it if necessary. Keep those thoughts trapped inside where they belong. Because if the imp of the mind ever sidesteps the body and gets its hand directly on the steering wheel, humankind can look forward to six months of unpredictable chaos, then doomsday.</p></div></div>
<div id="article-body-blocks"><span class="inline">
                <img src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/About/General/2011/4/8/1302285162577/Wired-to-computers-001.jpg" alt="Wired to computers" width="210" height="330" />
        </a>        			<span class="caption" style="width: 210px;">
				Resist the mind probe. Thicken your skull. Staple a doormat around it if necessary. Photograph: Getty Images/Science Photo Library 
			</span>
            </span>
<p>Ever since about 1998, when humankind began fast- forwarding through the gradually-unfolding history of progress, like someone impatiently zipping through a YouTube clip in search of the best bits, we've grown accustomed to machines veering from essential to  obsolete in the blink of a trimester. VHS, the Walkman, fax machines,  CD-Roms, pagers, dial-up modems . . . all consigned to the same wing of the museum housing the mangle and the horse-drawn plough.</p><p>The junk mountain grows by the day. If your home is anything like mine,  it contains several rarely explored  crannies stashed full of archaic chargers, defunct cables, and freshly antiquated gizmos whose sole useful function in 2011 is to make 2005 feel like 1926,  simply by looking big and dull and  impossibly lumpen. Everyone's opened a drawer and been startled by the  unexpected discovery of an old mobile phone that now resembles an outsized pantomime prop. To think you used to be impressed by this clunky breezeblock. You were like a caveman gawping at a yo-yo.</p><p>Now it's almost time to hurl another outmoded device down the historical garbage chute: your body. Last week, researchers at <a href="http://news.wustl.edu/news/Pages/21876.aspx" title="Washington University ">Washington University </a>unveiled a new mind-control computer system. Traditional mind-control  systems – and the fact that any mind-control system can be referred to as "traditional" shows you how nuts-deep into the future we already are –  require the user to don an EEG skullcap before thinking very hard about specific actions. The resultant brainwaves are then crudely interpreted and the  device reacts accordingly. But practical use is severely restricted thanks to the human skull, which muffles some  signals and amplifies others. It's like trying to work out what your neighbours are up to by pressing your ear against the wall: fun, but often wildly misleading.</p><p>Which is where <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrocorticography" title="electrocorticography">electrocorticography</a> comes in. Electrocorticography basically means "sticking sensors directly on to the surface of the brain". Once you've done that, you get a far more reliable signal. Already they've had volunteers controlling an onscreen cursor by imagining different vowel sounds. As soon as they refine it further, giving the user the ability to steer the pointer around and click on things, the days of mass-market Wi-Fi mind-controlled iPads will be upon us before you can smother your kids in their sleep to protect them from precisely such a future.</p><p>But is this really so sinister? All computers are mind-controlled already. My hand may steer the mouse and my fingers may punch the keys, but none of this takes place without my mental say-so. My brain runs things round here. Surely all a mind-controlled  interface does is cut out the corporeal middleman, leaving your fingers free to do something more useful, such as plugging your ears so you can't hear the horrified screams spontaneously exploding from your facehole? What's the problem?</p><p>The problem is that the body is the final, crucial buffer between the skittish human mind and the slavish machine servant. Think of how many furious email responses you've composed in haste, only to halt and reflect at the final moment as your finger hovers over the "send" button. The simple fact that a small physical action is required to actually deliver the damn thing is often enough to give pause for thought.</p><p>When mind-controlled computers become a commonplace reality, you'll have typed and sent that message in the time it takes to stub a toe; as quick as pulling a facial expression, but more detailed, and full of swearwords.</p><p>And while your brain might be great at controlling machines, how great are you at controlling your brain? What if, 10 years in the future, you're watching a cartoon on your futuristic 3D computer television, and the cartoon's got a rabbit in it, and the rabbit's slightly coquettish and flirty, and the knowing way it flicks that cotton tail as it hops makes you think about sex momentarily, and before you know it, your brain's retrieved some disgraceful bestial rabbit porn from the very worst corners of the Ultranet, and is relaying it on the display in lurid 96-inch holographic guttervision just as your wife and kid come back from the shops? And then, drunk on self-destructive power, your computer-mind takes a four-second video-snapshot of your own child's horrified gasping face and mischievously scribbles a load of  penises and swastikas all over it, and uploads this vandalised looping portrait to your 3D holographic Facebook page accompanied by a headline screaming WITNESS MY NADIR – JUDGE ME! JUDGE ME! JUDGE ME ONE AND ALL!</p><p>Let's face it, if you're honest, there's a whole world of shit routinely fizzing and popping around in your head that you wouldn't want a computer to unquestioningly act on. Remember: when they triumphantly unveil an iPhone that lets you dial your sweetheart simply  by thinking about their face, don't be fooled into thinking it's wonderful. It's a slippery slope. Resist the mind probe. Thicken your skull. Staple a doormat around it if necessary. Keep those thoughts trapped inside where they belong.  Because if the imp of the mind ever sidesteps the body and gets its hand  directly on the steering wheel, humankind can look forward to six months of unpredictable chaos, then doomsday.</p></div>
</div>

And the bright promise of cybersex - whatever happened to that? By now we were meant to be squeezing into crotch-hugging suits for well, crotch-hugging from an avatar resembling Jane Fonda in <i>Barbarella</i>. Maybe this mind control stuff should be viewed as a hopeful sign these other things are on the way.</p><p>I think Charlie puts his digital digit on it when he mentions the imp of the mind - or The Imp Of The Perverse in Poe's phrase. We are not in control of our own mind to start with because we have the subconscious squatting in it - our dark twin that throws up the opposite of every thought and feeling in a never-ending mental static we strain to tune out. What we actually think, say, do is the resultant of all the conflicts that the brain computer resolves in nanoseconds leaving us almost unaware of the process. The frontal lobes or wherever we put the inhibitory centres the last time we used them understand this game and routinely ignore or don't take seriously this stream of contrary consciousness - but machines will act on it. </p><p>Transactions online insist on treating humans like cold, calculating computers themselves whereas we all know we are these frightened animals with electro-wet stuff inside that is constantly crackling with random thought signals and base reason-trashing emotions such as fear, rage, lust and errm...more fear. Yet if we make computers more emotional we're into <i>Space Odyssey</i> territory with psycho-cyborgs and more Tate Modern black obelisk installations. Oh, what a tangled worldwide web we weave...</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10318920);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10318920">29</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10318920"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10318920" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10318920" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10318930"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/Kaitain/ffed997a-8ac2-4d8f-8f5f-314a693fe28b/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Kaitain" title="User profile page">Kaitain</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 1:56AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>I have little doubt than within fifty years most of us will be spending most hours of most days inside The Matrix. </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/Kaitain/ffed997a-8ac2-4d8f-8f5f-314a693fe28b/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Kaitain" title="User profile page">Kaitain</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 2:03AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <blockquote><p>The problem is that the body is the final, crucial buffer between the skittish human mind and the slavish machine servant. Think of how many furious email responses you've composed in haste, only to halt and reflect at the final moment as your finger hovers over the "send" button. The simple fact that a small physical action is required to actually deliver the damn thing is often enough to give pause for thought.</p></blockquote><p>But every time you move your arm, it's controlled by your mind. So you're just faced with an infinite regress.</p><p>Consider this: your arms and legs are controlled directly by your mind, with no barrier between the two. Do you find your arms and legs thrashing spasmodically because of this lack of filtering? No. You learn to control them despite the flittering nature of your mind. Same thing would apply with direct neural control of a computer.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10318947);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10318947">10</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10318947"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10318947" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10318947" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10318950"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/moonview/ba7e7d0e-d5b6-4220-899b-bc32f2a6e97f/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/moonview" title="User profile page">moonview</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 2:04AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>The only way out is to fast forward to the time when our natural, organic neurones connect up so completely that we'll be able to read telepathically every thought on the surface of the ever advancing ganglia anyway. It is being developed for quadraplegics and people with locked in syndrome. A man previously thought to have PVS was found to have awareness when ECG monitoring was used and he was asked to imagine walking through one room for yes and another place for no. You can get a lot of information from yes, no questions and it turned out he was aware, and wanted to live. </p><p>This technique is designed to give people like him more than yes, no questions and some degree of autonomy. Charlie is just concerned about his own obsolescence. After all how weak will his 'horrified at life' schtick look when The Mail has a mysanthrope with locked in syndrome and a net connection writing for them? </p><p>But more seriously, you would be mad to let anyone put wires in your head unless you were like that or had Parkinson's so bad the drugs don't work (we already do this). This is because every time you expose the brain you risk infection, every wire in/on the brain exiting through the skull is a conduit down which bacteria can travel. On that risk-benefit analysis alone do want this?</p><p>Finally recording from the surface of the brain and mind reading are two entirely different things, despite Charlie's lurid fears. To use his own analogy, you have just scraped some plaster from your side of the wall and you are still sitting there with your ear glued to it. You haven't laced the neighbours with HD 3D cameras from every possible angle.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10319569);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10319569">23</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10319569"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10319569" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10319569" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10319578"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2011/04/01/jaapdenhaan/300ac7f4-98c1-4b9e-b5c0-d355e0a77834/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/jaapdenhaan" title="User profile page">jaapdenhaan</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 7:44AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Barrier</p><blockquote><p>Jesus Chirst. </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/09/02/cta_262190_08/878be48c-e80a-4420-bc84-0a1b0ca58370/60x60.png" class="comment-body"> <blockquote><p>Resist the mind probe. Thicken your skull. Staple a doormat around it if necessary.</p></blockquote><p>Others have made the connection with a tinfoil hat. I prefer to call mine a Mind Burkha or Mind Niqab. Then when others laugh, I can sue them. Unlike the risible tinfoil hat wearers.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10319807);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10319807">4</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10319807"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10319807" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10319807" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10319817"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/everchanging" title="User profile page">everchanging</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 8:32AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>My nephew was once playing on his Gameboy-thingy rubbish and I asked him about it. "Look", I said, "Are you really playing this thing or is it playing you?"<br/>"What do you mean?", he said. The young fellow was at psychology A-level so was interested. "Well, you bought it which is what the manfacturers wanted you to do, you keep buying the latest games and you keep playing it endlessly. Surely you are doing what they want? Think of all of he other things you could be doing today instead! They are playing you! He got it.<br/>I think he dropped it not long after. I felt a bit of a spoilsport though.</p><p>David Bohm, the great physicist and philosopher, made some great comments along these lines saying that largely <b>thought is controlling us </b> not the other way round. Thought acts in such a way to look after us (or directing us in this Nature-free world we live in) and acts so fast that it seems like independently it is us who are in charge. The only way out of this is to watch the thought develop and hold it without acting on it. Hold it just there and analyse it, see exactly where it came from and then decide on the necessity of acting on it. Then you have freedom of a sort because you are no longer slavishly, skitterishly acting on your thoughts.</p><p>And if we are constantly being wacked by images telling us to buy this and that and through this new thing you will be better off, then you are in the trap that this <b>consumerist thought </b> has over of you. You have to stop and say really, really, do I need this crap. A lot of it is crap really.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10319817);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10319817">23</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10319817"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10319817" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10319817" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10319827"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/callitwhatitis" title="User profile page">callitwhatitis</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 8:34AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>The average conspiracy theorist thinks they are all out to get him. Think of the fun he (it's usually a he) could have with that one.</p><p>My girlfriend is horrified by the thought of my brainwaves instantly being relayed on to anything. She is of the opinion that the more stumbling blocks that are put in the wax of my utterances, the longer it will take for frown creases to permanently set in on her face.</p> </div> </li> <li No, they cannot.<br/></p></blockquote><p>Humans are machines...</p><p>And in any case the question is not as simply answered as you suppose. Whole schools of psychology and philosophy are devoted to the question. I don't agree with them, but a hard-core behaviourist like Gilbert Ryle would tell you that if something can mimick our outward behaviour entirely then any discussion of it not being concious is meaningless, concious being an illusion.</p><blockquote><p>A clear case of a hyperventilating article. ANd why must it 'translate neuronic activity into conciousness'? All that would be fundamentally necessary is for a piece of equipment to be programmed to produce brainwaves which have an influence on the mind.</p><p>But all this is irrelevant since the article was meant to be a bit of fun, although Brooker strangely seems closer to appreciating the possibilities than you are.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10319873);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10319873">6</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10319873"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10319873" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10319873" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10319927"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/muscleguy/96e0cf25-070c-4def-a18b-ec09ef314dcc/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/muscleguy" title="User profile page">muscleguy</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 8:48AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>@Flyswatter</p><blockquote><p>There is no way that a computer can translate neuronic activity into consciousness--unless the computer itself can acquire consciousness. This is not possible. There is an ongoing mystery about consciousness that neuroscience just cannot explain.</p></blockquote><p>Except that we are already doing this, in the case of the man with PVS I mentioned earlier. But you keep your head stuck in the hole in the ground, shivering in fear at what is happening in science and chanting your mantra: it can't happen, it can't happen, it can't happen. </p><p>Maybe you won't notice when the modern world rolls right over the top of you.</p><p>Oh and New Scientist had an article last week on a computer/robot system that was set up like our conscious/subconscious system with the data handled by the 'subconscious' systems having to win a quorum battle with the others before being passed to the 'conscious' system on top. It had the same reaction times in tests that humans do. I am afraid that Occam's Razor sliced off too much here.</p><p>Again, only physiological brains can replicate themselves. A non-physiological machine can certainly do things it is programmed for but it just cannot <b>know</b>what it is doing. Thus the idea of having one's neuronic activities channeled through a machine then splashed on a screen is interesting, but scientifically fictitious nevertheless. In other words the relaying machine would have to acquire self-consciousness to do just that. I just doubt it could--unless it were a replica of a physiological brain.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10320596);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10320596">5</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10320596"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10320596" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10320596" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10320611"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/muscleguy/96e0cf25-070c-4def-a18b-ec09ef314dcc/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/muscleguy" title="User profile page">muscleguy</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 9:59AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>@PietroMercurios</p><p>'Knowledge', look it up. </p><p>I hesitate to make a an argument from authority but I am a research biologist with more than just a passing knowledge of Neuroscience. I posit that we're driven by evolution and anything else is just humanist snobbery.</p><p>There appears to be an overarching theme at work i.e. the creation of a central consciousness. This is obviously going to take a bit of a while yet. We're certainly not really able to control the technology responsibly at this stage but fairly confident that it will happen. The internet was created out of an unconscious desire to merge. Is it a buddhistic desire for global enlightenment? A quantum leap of consciousness? A desire for have access to good quality free hardcore porn?</p><p>We've just got have a sense of scale about it. Evolution works to it's own glacial time (however the addition of technology into the mix will certainly speed things up somewhat - have a read of Ray Kurzweil on that subject). The only weak link in this is energy. At present the whole lot would stop dead in it's tracks without energy and then we'd all be living in Cormac McCarthy's 'The Road'.</p><p>To be Post-human requires batteries.</p> </div> </li> <li the imp of the mind ever sidesteps the body and gets its hand directly on the steering wheel</p></blockquote><p>Once you've learned to drive, effectively this is exactly what happens. See bend coming, steer, totally unaware of actually turning the steering wheel.</p><p>Pretending for a moment that I'm posting on a serious website and not CIF, CB is a long way behind modern thought. There is no "mind" and "body". You cannot take the "mind" out and have the body stand there like a zombie. The nervous system is like a fan, with lower and lower level functions being controlled as you get further from whatever is the "rational" bit of the brain, but the supposedly rational bit being affected in turn by those lower functions and emotions. Any idea of abstracting one from the other is rather silly. What these remote controllers are doing is picking up the signals that are moving around the nervous system. No nervous system, no signal.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10322947);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10322947">3</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10322947"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10322947" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10322947" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10323077"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Kyza06" title="User profile page">Kyza06</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 12:47PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <blockquote><p>@sentience - agreed. For example, when working out a sum, we might count on our fingers or on an abacus. When we do this, the physical action is part of the mental process of working out the sum. Our fingers and the abacus become for all <b>functional</b> intents and purposes, part of our mind. The brain forms a large part of our mind, but in fact everything we use to work things out, remember things, perform various actions, is part of our mind. Part of my mind may even exist inside someone else's head. </p><p>We might be able to control a computer directly with our brain, but if we accept the extended mind hypothesis, we might actually be losing an important part of our mental capacity. I guess this is what Charlie meant when he talked about the physical action of clicking on something being enough to make you think again. </p><p>&lt;a href=The Extended Mind - Andy Clark and David Chalmers&gt;http://consc.net/papers/extended.html</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10323137);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10323137">1</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10323137"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10323137" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10323137" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10323146"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Jakpaddy" title="User profile page">Jakpaddy</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 12:50PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Note... And like it or not, they'll just leave you behind.</i> (Wordsmith20)</p><p>I've just been catching up on the news and I've read a few reports about a fatal stabbing in London. A recording of a track made by the victim and his friends in which they rap about shanks (knives), money, beefs (feuds), straps (guns) and karma.</p><p>In the future new mind-control computer technology could enable people to post threats online without letting go of their shanks.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10326259);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10326259">1</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10326259"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10326259" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10326259" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10326548"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/LittleRichardjohn/326adcb7-1740-429d-b304-3c2b0779544b/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/LittleRichardjohn" title="User profile page">LittleRichardjohn</a> <p class="date">11 April 2011 4:04PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Let's take an old school bit of desktop software like Photoshop. Would we have real OS wars, as opposed to the present OS flame wars?</p><p>Would the nomenclatura operate under Mac OS CXX, unterpunters stagger along with Win 22 Turbo whilst future tee-peeistes, real wierdos still argue the toss a GUI flavoured or ethnic command-line existence.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10330693);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10330693">0</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10330693"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10330693" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10330693" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10332349"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/10/04/SteB1/2ef11f83-d0ee-4f51-bef7-9080ce072940/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/SteB1" title="User profile page">SteB1</a> <p class="date">12 April 2011 12:28AM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>The whole idea that electronic devices "controlled" by certain patterns of brain activitiy, are directly reading our thoughts, is a load of baloney . These devices are no more reading our minds or thoughts, than is a conventional keyboard operated computer. Just because certain patterns of brain activity are approximately associated with certain thoughts or feelings, does not indicate that there is some sort of known or discoverable signal, or code system in the mind, which can be precisely read. This whole belief is a fantasist myth of techno futurists. It is like believing that because someone's face goes red when they are embarrassed, that the feelings of embarrassment are located in the face. It is the classical error of confusing a correlation, with a cause. It is caused by erroneous presupposition that because computers are controlled by programs written in code, that the mind operates in an identical way. And that once this code is dicovered, Bob's your uncle.</p><p>We need to separate the concept of the mind and the brain. Not separate brain and mind, <b>but concepts of brain and mind</b>, which are not the same thing as actual brain and mind. Ideas and presuppositions about brain and mind, are not one and the same as actual brain and mind. It is another classical error, mistaking the map for the territory i.e. confusing the idea with the phenomena it refers to.</p><p>I am not saying that the mind is some separate soul like thing with no connection to the brain. It is just ridiculously over simplistic to think of the brain as some sort of organic computer run by some sort of intelligible code. The notion that there is a body controlled by a brain, is quite clearly a contrivance, because many organisms do not have brains, but they still behave in a coordinated way, and are capable of complex responses, without there being a brain organising these actions. A brain would be nothing without the rest of the body, which is also involved in what we feel. It is just an assumption that there is a bit of our brain inhabited by some sort of homonculus controlling us - our <b>self</b>. In fact it is really a type of theological belief based in ideas of a soul. Other cultures did not see it like this. We are confusing our cultural myth that there is some sort of central "soul" - located somewhere in our brain - with the actuality. There is no scientific basis for these beliefs, it is all cultural presuppostition i.e. myth.</p><p>Even the whole pattern of something that an electronic computer does, and produces, cannot understood on the basis of its parts, the basic code itself. A CD or a DVD can contain the code for a computer to produce music, movies, games, written documents etc. However the "essential whole" of these complex patterns of things the computer can produce, such as musical arrangements, are not to be found in the simplistic bumps the laser scans on the CD/DVD. The majority of books written in the English language can be written with 26 letters, 10 numerals, a few punctuation signs, and a few mathematical symbols. Only specialised books about maths, music, science etc, need a few more symbols.</p><p>Yet can we understand Chaucer, Shakespeare, all the other writers, all philisophy, all ideas etc, with a simplistic understanding of these basic symbols of the alphabet? Of course not, it would be a stupid presumption to believe we could. Because the essence of what is written, is not be found in these 26 letters. It is only in the higher level arrangement of these symbols, that meaningful complexity emerges.</p><p><b>So where do we get the incredibly stupid idea that by reading the patterns created by firing neurons and bloodflow in the brain - that somehow we can decode our thoughts, ideas, feelings etc?</b></p><p>This myth above is a presumption so utterly crass and stupid, that you have to wonder how any half intelligent human being could ever believe such a gigantic crock of shit. Because if you can't understand a great work of literature, or philosophy, by understanding the alphabet, then how the hell does anyone believe that you can understand the mind by the firing of neorons and bloodflow in the brain? It is a profoundly stupid presumption and myth. We believe this pile of crap, simply because some clever person told us it was the case, because it gives the impression that they understand far more than they actually do.</p><p>Any human mind is incredibly complex. A neuron can only fire, or not fire i.e. 2 possible states. So where does this stupid myth come from, that via a grossly simplistic reductionist analysis of neuron firing and bloodflow patterns in the brain, that we can decode thoughts and feelings?</p><p>We believe this stupid idea, because a clever man in a white coat told us that is how it is! Why don't we think for ourselves?<br/><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milgram_experiment" rel="nofollow">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milgram_experiment</a></p> </div> </li> <li your long and eloquently written post still contains the argument 'Mind is sacred', so you're still ultimately arguing for the existance of something 'other' that makes up consciousness and the concept of 'mind'.</p><p>These machines certainly don't read whole mindstates - i.e. the complete pattern of the mind as it changes from micro-second to microsecond - however, unless you are a Platonist or otherwise believe in soul or similar, ultimately human consicousness is a function of the ordering of physical matter into specific patterns. Given time, the right kind of measuring kit and statistical cross-referencing, you could decode a person's emotional state from the various biochemical patterns in the brain (not just neurons &amp; bloodflow, but the whole gamut of biochemical processes that happen in the brain).</p><p>I.e., if you scan enough people crying when they're sad., you can build a model that says 'This is what the brain looks like when someone is sad' and so on. Your example of how we can look at something simple (the alphabet) and combine it into something higher is <i>exactly</i> what I've described - taking simple things (neurons, bloodflow, hormonal levels, magnetic activity etc etc) and combining them to make a greater whole that means something.</p><p>So are you invoking soul (or similar), or are you saying that mind is irreducibly complex?</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10337190);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10337190">1</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10337190"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main" /> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10337190" /> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks" /> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10337190" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10339929"> <li class="comment-author" > <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/campasyoulike" title="User profile page">campasyoulike</a> <p class="date">12 April 2011 2:47PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Ok. </div> <div class="profile" > <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/10/04/SteB1/2ef11f83-d0ee-4f51-bef7-9080ce072940/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt="" /> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/SteB1" title="User profile page">SteB1</a> <p class="date">12 April 2011 6:13PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>@Kyza06</p><blockquote><p>SteB1 - your long and eloquently written post still contains the argument 'Mind is sacred', so you're still ultimately arguing for the existance of something 'other' that makes up consciousness and the concept of 'mind'.</p><p>These machines certainly don't read whole mindstates - i.e. the complete pattern of the mind as it changes from micro-second to microsecond - however, unless you are a Platonist or otherwise believe in soul or similar, ultimately human consicousness is a function of the ordering of physical matter into specific patterns. Given time, the right kind of measuring kit and statistical cross-referencing, you could decode a person's emotional state from the various biochemical patterns in the brain (not just neurons &amp; bloodflow, but the whole gamut of biochemical processes that happen in the brain).</p><p>I.e., if you scan enough people crying when they're sad., you can build a model that says 'This is what the brain looks like when someone is sad' and so on. Your example of how we can look at something simple (the alphabet) and combine it into something higher is exactly what I've described - taking simple things (neurons, bloodflow, hormonal levels, magnetic activity etc etc) and combining them to make a greater whole that means something.</p><p>So are you invoking soul (or similar), or are you saying that mind is irreducibly complex?<br/></p></blockquote><p>I'm not really invoking anything. Rather I was pointing out that the whole idea of the reading of our thoughts, feelings etc, is rubbish. For a start the way our language and culture divides up mental experiences into thoughts, feelings, emotions, moods, a concept of self is itself a complete contrivance. I have certainly never experienced these discrete experiences of thoughts, feelings and emotions, in the way that these concepts of them imply must exist. The words and concpets we use to describe our mental experiences are just crude labels to make sense of something more complex for the sake of trying to communicate what is going on. Just because I say I had a thought, does not necessarily mean that some discrete identifiable thing called a thought just occurred in my mind.</p><p>You cannot read a thought, when no one has ever satisfactorily described or explained what a thought is.</p><p>I'm certainly saying that mind is irreducably complex. However, saying this is not invoking some religious view. It is actually a quasi religious view, derived from theology, that we can untangle and understand all this. People like Newton believed that they were revealing god's laws and thinking. Ultimately, common sense realism is based upon theological speculation and faith, not on empericisim. Even matter is irreducably complex. What physics reveals about the basis of matter, is something very different than everyday common sense conceptions of what matter is.</p><blockquote><p>ultimately human consicousness is a function of the ordering of physical matter into specific patterns. Given time, the right kind of measuring kit and statistical cross-referencing, you could decode a person's emotional state from the various biochemical patterns in the brain (not just neurons &amp; bloodflow, but the whole gamut of biochemical processes that happen in the brain).</p></blockquote><p>These are faith based assumptions. The idea that the arrangement of matter creates human consciousness, is an article of faith, and not based upon any science. It is <b>process</b> that matters, not the "ordering of physical matter". You might have 2 very similar fresh fish lying on a slab in front of you, one dead, the other still alive. If you put them back in the water, only one would swim off, and the other would rot. Yet the pattern of the physical matter in both would be very similar. What is missing in one is <b>process</b>. It's dead, because something happened to it which meant that the <b>process</b> of life could no longer continue, not because it lost any vital spark. Once stopped this process does not restart. The patter of life is circular <b>process</b>. Souls and vitalism are just mistaken concepts, that don't have much to do with how things actually are. I can know that an idea is mistaken, when it is inconsistent with how things are, without actually knowing myself, the answer to everything.</p><blockquote><p>These machines certainly don't read whole mindstates</p></blockquote><p>These machines don't ready any mindstate, partial or whole.<br/><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room" rel="nofollow">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room</a></p><blockquote><p>if you scan enough people crying when they're sad., you can build a model that says</p></blockquote><p><br/>You are mistaking the model for what it models, mistaking the map for the territory. No matter how good a conceptual model is, it is never the phenomena that it models.</p><p>This guy's worth reading.<br/><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Grand" rel="nofollow">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Grand</a></p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend"
